- **Friday — blade work.** Sword and knife. The fencing fundamentals are still there from the teenage training; she layers Filipino stick/knife flow over them. Shorter session, usually forty-five minutes, because it's mostly maintenance.
- **Saturday — long session, mixed, often with a partner.** When Clint is in town this is shared. When America is around this is shared. When she is alone this is the longest day — three hours, frequently outdoor, frequently with run-and-shoot drills.
- **Sunday — rest, light yoga, mobility.** She is religious about rest day in a way she is religious about nothing else. A surveillance file would show *zero* Sunday training sessions over a representative quarter.

Equipment maintenance is itself a ritual, performed Sunday evenings: she inspects every arrow in her primary quiver, replaces fletching as needed, checks the bowstring for wear, waxes it. The kit is laid out on a clean dishtowel on the kitchen counter. She does this while watching television, usually something low-stakes (cooking shows, old procedurals). The combination — lethal tools and ambient television — is one of the most diagnostic images of her domestic life.

---

### 7.4 Lucky

The dog is not a pet. The dog is **infrastructure, companion, and primary daily attachment object.** Surveillance would record the following as fixed and inviolable:

- **06:50 (approx) — morning walk.** Twenty minutes. Same route most days, a small loop she varies by one block every few weeks to keep the dog interested. She carries bags, water, and (in pockets) one pen and one folding knife. The phone is on but face-down in the pocket; she does not look at it on the walk.
- **Pizza is a load-bearing food group for the dog.** She buys it for him. She splits it with him. She has been observed eating one slice and giving him three.
- **The dog sleeps on the bed.** Specifically against her left hip, against the side of the body she does not draw from. This is unconscious tactical accommodation. She has never relocated him.
- **Mid-day check-ins.** If she is out for more than four hours and the dog is at home, she returns or sends someone. Cases get reorganized around the dog's bladder. Clients have been rescheduled.
- **Evening: long walk, 35–60 minutes,** usually right before dusk, beach-adjacent when LA, river-adjacent when New York. This is **her decompression window** and the only daily ritual she protects more fiercely than training. Phone goes to silent. She talks to the dog audibly, in full sentences. A long-lens audio operator would capture her processing the day aloud in this window — and would learn more about her interior than any other surveillance vector would yield.

---

### 7.5 Habits of Place

**The car.** A beat-up coupe in the LA era, treated as a mobile office. Glove compartment contains: registration, a granola bar of indeterminate age, a small first-aid kit (actually stocked, not decorative), a backup phone charger, a folded twenty-dollar bill, and a paperback she has been "halfway through" for months. Trunk contains a duffel with a change of clothes, a collapsible bow case, two quivers, and a pair of running shoes. The car is washed irregularly and the interior is clean but cluttered with coffee cups she means to throw out and doesn't.

**The apartment.** Minimalist by inheritance-hoarding standards, lived-in by minimalist standards. Books *everywhere* — paperbacks stacked horizontally on shelves because the shelves are full vertically. Mysteries, surveillance memoirs, poetry (a small surprising shelf), one shelf of art books inherited from her father's collection that she keeps despite their provenance because they are good. The walls have art she chose, not art she inherited. The kitchen is under-utilized; she cooks rarely and competently when she does. The fridge contents are a tell: if it is empty, she is in a good week (eating out, working hard, mobile); if it is full of leftovers, she is depressed or convalescing.

**The bed.** Made daily, hospital-corner-tight. This is **military-grade self-discipline** and a residual from the post-trauma reconstruction of self. She has admitted it to Clint once. The unmade bed is a clinical sign — when surveillance records it, something is wrong.

**The bathroom.** Counter has: one toothbrush, toothpaste, floss, a hair tie, a single tube of moisturizer, a single lipstick (which she wears rarely and reliably to family functions). The medicine cabinet contains ibuprofen, arnica gel (constant), a small bottle of melatonin (occasional), no prescription psychotropics, no birth control visible (which suggests either none or discreet storage; surveillance would not resolve this).

**The range and the gym.** She is recognized but not fussed-over at both. She tips the range attendant at Christmas. She has a locker at the gym with her own gi and a spare set of wraps. She does not socialize after sessions. She showers at home.

---

### 7.6 Phone, Comms, and Information Hygiene

**Two phones.** Personal and work, both iPhones in the modern era, the work phone older and more disposable. She has had at least three burners across documented arcs and treats them as consumables.

She is **disciplined about location services** — off by default on the personal phone, on for the work phone during active cases. She does not post to social media in real time. The Hawkeye Investigations account, when it exists, is run by Ramone or a friend; Kate writes captions and refuses to take selfies. She has a personal account she barely uses; the last several posts are usually of the dog.

She reads news in the morning while standing at the kitchen counter. She reads two papers (NYT and LA Times in alternation), one local alt-weekly when she can find it, and an inconsistent rotation of long-form magazines. She is meaningfully literate; this is undersold by the persona.

Texting style: **lowercase, fast, generous with emoji to friends, terse and properly capitalized with clients and Avengers business.** Voice memos to Clint; full emails to America (long-distance/multiversal makes calls hard); phone calls to her sister rare and short. To Eleanor: nothing she can avoid; everything she sends is reviewed twice before sending.

---

### 7.7 Eating, Drinking, and the Pizza Question

Eats like a working person, not a wealthy person. Has favorite cheap places and revisits them in stable rotation — the constancy is part of the comfort. Knows the names of the staff. Tips well. Brings the dog where the dog is allowed and brings food back where he isn't.

Cooks rarely; what she cooks is good. Eggs many ways, pasta, a competent roast chicken, one Korean dish she learned from a Young Avengers-era friend, one Indian dish she learned from Clint's family of strays. Bakes never.

Alcohol: beer with Clint, wine with America, cocktails on rare occasions with her sister or at industry events she cannot avoid. **Two-drink ceiling held with notable consistency.** A third drink is a tell that something specific is happening — usually that she has just talked to her mother. She does not drink alone at home except, occasionally, half a beer with the dishes.

Pizza, as noted, is for the dog. She eats it but resents how much of it she eats because of him.

---

### 7.8 Sleep

**Sleeps well by trauma-survivor standards** and noticeably better than her mentor. Six and a half to eight hours typical. Falls asleep within fifteen minutes of lights-out, a function of the day's physical load. Dreams: not extensively pathologized in canon. She has admitted to a recurring archery-related anxiety dream (arrow won't fly, bow won't draw) and to one specific Central Park dream that she does not describe.

She sleeps on her side, left side, dog at the hip. She does not sleep with the bow in the bedroom; it lives in a case in the next room. She sleeps with one knife in the nightstand drawer and has done since she was nineteen. She has never used it from that position. Surveillance would record her checking the drawer is closed before sleeping — a small, unconscious gesture, hand brushing the handle of the drawer once.

She wakes once or twice in the night to drink water, never to check her phone. If she wakes a third time, she gets up, walks the dog (if it is past 05:30), or reads in the kitchen until the urge to sleep returns. **She does not lie in bed awake.** This is a deliberate trained behavior and a strong protective factor.

---

### 7.9 Weekly and Monthly Rhythms

**Weekly:**
- *Sunday* — rest, equipment maintenance, long walk with the dog, cooking for the week (sometimes), a long call with Clint (sometimes), a film.
- *Monday–Friday* — work and training as above.
- *Saturday* — long training session, social plans in the evening, the occasional concert or museum (she is a museum person — quiet, slow, alone, takes notes).

**Monthly:**
- A check-in with a small circle of women: Cassie when reachable, America when she's on this side of whichever multiverse, Jessica Jones in some continuities, sometimes Bobbi Morse.
- A scheduled archery competition or demo when she can fit one — she does not compete for ranking, she competes to test herself in unfamiliar conditions.
- An obligatory family contact (sister Susan most reliably; mother when she can't dodge it). She schedules these for the same week to absorb the cost in one stretch.
- One day, roughly, of nothing — no work, no training, no social — usually the day after an Avengers-level event or a hard case close. She calls it "the decompression."

**Annually:**
- *Birthday.* Low-key. Dinner with Clint or America; she does not throw parties for herself.
- *The anniversary of the assault.* Not marked in text but inferable from the rhythm of certain arcs — she becomes quieter, trains harder, picks up a heavier case load for a week or two. A surveillance file across years would isolate the date by signal.
- *Holidays.* Avoids the Bishop family Christmas when she can. Spends it instead with Clint's family, the Young Avengers, or Lucky and a movie.

---

### 7.10 The Micro-Tells

What a long-lens operator would catalogue across a quarter:

- **The rooftop glance** at every doorway she uses regularly. Habitual, unconscious, fast.
- **The drawer-handle brush** before sleep.
- **The two-finger tap on the bow grip** before her first shot of any session — pure ritual, possibly superstition, never skipped.
- **The eight-and-eight ankle rolls** at wake.
- **The under-breath count when threading a needle, fletching an arrow, or counting cash** — she counts in threes, audibly under breath, a tic from childhood.
- **The half-smile she gives bad clients and worse cops** that does not reach her eyes — distinct from the full smile she gives friends, which does.
- **The hand-to-the-back-of-the-neck gesture** when she is about to tell someone something she does not want to tell them. Reliable. Surveillance would catch it before the disclosure.
- **The pause before she answers a call from her mother** — average length, four to seven seconds, during which she stands still and looks at the phone. She answers about half the time. The other half she returns the call within twenty-four hours.
- **The way she eats while reading** — book in left hand, fork in right, never the other way, even though she is mixed-dominant in combat. Reading is a left-hand activity for her.
- **The dog's name as punctuation** — she says "Lucky" the way some people say "okay," using it as a verbal reset between thoughts when she is alone or with the dog. A surveillance audio file would clock this dozens of times per day in her domestic space.

---

### 7.11 What a Week Would Look Like, In Total

A representative seven-day surveillance log would show: forty-five to fifty-five waking hours of work; nine to twelve hours of formal training; eight to ten hours with the dog in dedicated walks (more in ambient time); twelve to twenty hours of reading, watching, listening, decompressing; one to three social engagements, mostly small, mostly with the same five or six people; one family-of-origin contact, usually unwelcome; six to nine restaurant meals, most at the same handful of places; zero conspicuous consumption; zero substance impairment; zero unaccounted overnight absences absent active case-work; one or two minor injuries (bruises, knuckle abrasions, a strained wrist) carried without complaint.

The total picture is **a life of extraordinarily high structural integrity for someone in her line of work** — built, deliberately, brick by brick, from the rubble of a family and the residue of an assault, by someone who decided early that the inside of a day was hers to design.

She designs it well. Surveillance would, over time, come to respect it.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Subject: Katherine Elizabeth Bishop. Section scope: blood relations, household-of-origin attachments, and the dead who structure her interior life. Each entry below is constructed as a longitudinal file: behavioral baseline, observed evolution across the publication record, trust topography, characteristic failure mode, and the leverage profile a hostile actor (or a benevolent one) would derive from the relationship. The file is concerned with what is actually there, not what the subject says is there; in this family, the gap between those two is the entire diagnostic.*

---

## 5.1 DEREK BISHOP — Father

**Status across record.** Living, then antagonist, then dead, then reframed in retrospect. Across two decades of stories he moves from background-absent-patriarch (Heinberg's *Young Avengers*) to confirmed criminal launderer with organized-crime adjacency (Fraction's *Hawkeye*, Lemire's *All-New Hawkeye*) to direct adversary in Kate's solo arcs.

**Behavioral baseline.** The father presented Kate, throughout her childhood, with a specific and damaging combination: material saturation paired with attentional famine. The Bishop household ran on staff. Birthdays were catered. The father appeared at events as a photograph appears in a frame — present, posed, not interactive. Subject's earliest behavioral adaptation to him is the small-child performance of competence: she learned, before she was ten, that the most reliable way to earn his momentary attention was to be *impressive* in a way that reflected back on him. The Hawkingbird phase — the childhood archery games, the bow in the closet — likely originates here. It was a bid.

**Evolution.** The bid stopped paying out around adolescence. By the time of the wedding-day hostage event (YA #1), Kate's affect toward her father is already a hard ironic shell: she addresses him with formal courtesy and zero warmth, the register of a junior employee who has decided her boss is contemptible but will not yet say so. The discovery of his criminality is not, for her, a *revelation* in the destabilizing sense; it is a *confirmation* of a suspicion she had been carrying for years without articulating. This is why she absorbs it without the dissociative shock pattern one would expect — she had already moved her trust elsewhere. The break, when it comes, is total and undramatic. She walks away from the money. She does not negotiate; she does not stage a confrontation scene; she simply ceases to be his daughter in any functional sense and goes to live in a car in Los Angeles. The behavior is closer to *amputation* than *rupture* — clean, planned, and final.

**Trust / safety topography.** Zero by the time of canonical entry; *negative* by the Fraction era, in the sense that any communication from Derek is treated as adversarial intelligence rather than personal contact. Notably, she does not fear him. The threat axis is operational, not somatic. He cannot hurt her with a look or a phrase; he can only hurt her by leveraging the institutions he owns. This is a meaningful distinction: her father did not occupy her body the way her mother does.

**Failure mode.** Hers, not his: an enduring *aesthetic* contempt for inherited wealth that occasionally overshoots into reverse-snobbery — eating expired food when she could eat normally, declining favors she could accept, performing scrappiness in front of people who would not have judged her either way. This is reaction formation against Derek, and it is the cleanest evidence that he still occupies psychic territory she would deny he occupies.

**Leverage.** Low. He is the rare family member who cannot be used against her, because she pre-grieved him before he became a villain. The only residual leverage vector is *the Bishop name itself* — surfacing publicly attached to his crimes, with hers in the same sentence. She is sensitive to this in a brand-protective way rather than a shame-grief way.

---

## 5.2 ELEANOR BISHOP — Mother

**Status across record.** Presented as dead (early YA). Returned alive (Thompson *Hawkeye* 2016, *West Coast Avengers* 2018–2019). Revealed across that span to have faked her death, run a criminal organization in parallel to Derek's, and attempted to instrumentalize Kate as both heir and asset. The relationship is the single most important interpersonal datum in the file.

**Behavioral baseline (pre-disappearance).** Eleanor was, by the subject's recollection, the warm parent. The one who read to her, who taught her, who gave her the framing — *you are extraordinary, you are mine, you are safe with me.* The mother's affection appears to have been real in expression and selective in deployment; she was the parent who *showed up* in the room, which in the Bishop house was a high bar. The childhood imprint is therefore not the typical neglected-rich-girl signature; Kate did not grow up unloved. She grew up specifically loved by one person and then that person disappeared.

**The death.** Eleanor's "death" occurs early enough in the subject's life that it functions as the foundational loss. The behavioral consequences are durable: an early-adolescent collapse of the household's emotional center, the father's further withdrawal, the subject's drift toward older-female substitute figures (the Mockingbird-staves acquisition in YA #1 is not random — she instinctively reaches for the gear of *adult women who fight*), and the calcification of a private operating belief that *love and disappearance are linked*. This is the engine of her counter-dependency more than any single other factor.

**The assault.** Occurs in this window — post-loss, pre-restoration. The mother is not there. The mother *cannot be told*. Subject processes the trauma alone, with a father who is not a candidate for disclosure and a sister who is not a candidate for anything. The decision to convert the trauma into training rather than into seeking-comfort is, in part, a function of *having no one to seek comfort from*. The mother's absence is therefore structurally implicated in the shape of the recovery.

**Return and revelation.** When Eleanor returns alive, the subject's response is not the joy a healthy reunion would produce; it is a *carefully managed* re-engagement, watchful, with the affect of someone who has been given a gift she does not entirely believe is hers to keep. She wants to believe. She does not entirely believe. As the evidence of Eleanor's criminality and manipulation accumulates across Thompson's run and the *West Coast Avengers* arc, the subject demonstrates her single clinically significant judgment failure: she repeatedly upgrades her mother's trustworthiness on insufficient evidence. She gives Eleanor chances Derek would never have gotten. She rationalizes. She defers confrontation. She *hopes*.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor knows what Kate needs and is willing to perform it. This is the relationship's particular toxicity — it is not the cold-mother pattern (Kate could metabolize that) but the *intermittently warm-and-instrumentalizing* pattern, which is the hardest variant for any survivor to disengage from. Eleanor offers, periodically, the exact affective texture of the pre-disappearance childhood; the subject is hungry for it in a way she will not name; Eleanor uses the hunger.

**Trust / safety topography.** Inconsistent and chronically miscalibrated in the subject's own favor — i.e., she trusts higher than the evidence warrants. The somatic register is the inverse: Kate's body knows. Observable in the published record as a stiffening when Eleanor enters a room, a tightening at the jaw, a verbal register that becomes slightly too smooth — the cadence of someone who is *performing ease*. This is one of the few interpersonal contexts in which the subject's affect-regulation visibly *works* rather than running silently.

**Failure mode.** Re-engagement. The door reopens. Each re-opening is rationalized in a way the subject would not accept from any other person in her life. If she were her own friend, she would intervene.

**Leverage.** Maximal. This is the single highest-yield pressure point in the entire file. An adversary who needs Kate Bishop's judgment compromised does not threaten her — they produce Eleanor, alive, in distress, asking for help. The subject's tactical clarity drops measurably in proximity to this stimulus. She knows this about herself and has, to her credit, begun in the most recent material to articulate it; awareness has not yet produced reliable counter-conditioning.

---

## 5.3 SUSAN BISHOP — Sister

**Status across record.** Living, peripheral, largely off-page after the YA #1–2 wedding sequence. Appears intermittently in solo-title flashbacks and family-event scenes.

**Behavioral baseline.** Susan is the *legible* Bishop daughter — the one who married into appropriate wealth, who performs the social register the family expects, who treats Kate's vocation as embarrassing and Kate's competence as an inconvenience. The wedding-day hostage event is the relationship's defining vignette: Kate steps in to save her sister's life and her sister's response is, on balance, irritated. The pattern recurs across appearances. Susan is incapable of receiving Kate accurately.

**Dynamics.** Subject treats Susan with a register of *patient amused tolerance* that masks real grief. The sister is, technically, the only other person who shared the Eleanor-disappearance and Derek-distance childhood; she is the only available co-witness. She is unwilling or unable to function as one. Kate's loneliness in the family-of-origin frame is partly this — she lost the only sibling who could have corroborated her reality, and lost her not to death but to incuriosity.

**Evolution.** Static. Susan does not grow toward Kate; Kate does not pursue Susan. The relationship is in a stable cold equilibrium. No published material suggests rupture or repair; it simply persists at low temperature.

**Trust / safety.** Susan is not an active threat. She is also not a resource. She would not knowingly harm Kate; she would also not reliably warn Kate of harm coming from elsewhere in the family. She is *neutral terrain occupied by a person who shares a last name*.

**Failure mode.** Subject occasionally over-protects Susan in field-operational contexts where Susan does not warrant the risk. This is residual older-sister-by-temperament behavior in the absence of biographical seniority, and it is exploitable in the small.

**Leverage.** Low-to-moderate. Susan can be threatened and Kate will respond, but the response is duty-shaped rather than love-shaped — Kate would intervene to save her sister and would feel about it the way one feels about paying a bill that came due. An adversary who used Susan as bait would get Kate to the location; they would not get Kate's judgment scrambled the way Eleanor does.

---

## 5.4 THE HAWKINGBIRD CHILD — Earlier Self

*Treated here as a relationship because the subject treats it as one.*

**Status.** Internal object. Appears in the published record as flashback, as nickname, as a private register Kate uses with herself and very rarely shares.

**Behavioral baseline.** The Hawkingbird is the pre-trauma self — the child who chose the bow because it was fun, who made up a hero-name out of birds and mishearing, who had not yet been hurt. The subject's relationship to this prior self is unusually tender for her interpersonal register. She protects the Hawkingbird the way one protects a younger sibling. The childhood version is what she fights *for*, in a sense that includes but exceeds her assault-trauma framing: she is also restoring the kid's right to have meant it as a game.

**Dynamics.** Subject deploys the Hawkingbird memory as self-soothing in low-affect privacy; it is not a public artifact. In the rare moments she shares it (with Clint, late, in trust), it functions as a disclosure-equivalent to other survivors' disclosure of the assault itself. *The name is the wound spoken in code.*

**Failure mode.** Sentimentalization. Subject is mildly vulnerable to symbolic appeals that route through the childhood-bow imagery — purple, the original arrow-design, the hand-drawn target. An adversary with access to her childhood materials could produce affect-dysregulation she would not show in response to direct threat.

**Leverage.** Specialized and rare. Requires intelligence on the subject's pre-assault biography that almost no one possesses.

---

## 5.5 ELEANOR-AS-DEAD — The Ghost That Wasn't

*Functionally a separate relationship from the living Eleanor, and must be filed as such.*

**Status.** The internalized dead-mother object that the subject carried for the entire interval between Eleanor's faked death and Eleanor's return. This object did not dissolve when the mother reappeared; it persists in parallel.

**Behavioral baseline.** For most of Kate's developmental life, "my mother" referred to a person who was perfect and gone. The dead-mother was the idealized witness, the absent approver, the figure to whom achievements were privately dedicated. The subject's vocation, her name, her work — much of it was offered, at some level, *to* this figure. This is unremarkable for adult children of dead parents; what is remarkable in Kate's case is that the dead mother was, in fact, alive and watching.

**Dynamics.** The return of the living Eleanor *did not replace* the dead-mother object. The subject now carries both: the idealized internalized witness who loved her perfectly and is gone, and the live person who is neither of those things. Much of the cognitive error around the living Eleanor is, in fact, displacement *from* the dead-mother — the subject keeps trying to make the live person match the internal one, and keeps being disappointed because they are not the same entity and never were.

**Failure mode.** The dead-mother object cannot be argued with. It is pre-verbal and pre-evidence. The subject cannot use her excellent tactical reasoning against it; she can only become aware of it, which she partially has.

**Leverage.** Inaccessible to outside actors except via Eleanor herself.

---

## 5.6 DEREK-AS-FATHER-FIGURE — The Absent Template

**Status.** Internal object. The negative imprint that organizes Kate's response to *all subsequent older men*.

**Behavioral baseline.** Because Derek was emotionally unavailable rather than actively cruel, the subject's response to older-male authority figures is calibrated to a specific test: *will this person see me when I am in the room.* Steve Rogers passes the test (Captain America gives her the bow; sees her). Clint Barton passes the test in a particular way (sees her, also needs her, which the subject reads as a category of being-seen). Norman Osborn-type authority fails the test by definition. The Derek-template is the *measuring stick* against which other men are evaluated, and the standard is low enough that anyone who clears basic attentiveness gets credit.

**Failure mode.** Subject occasionally overweights the significance of being seen by older men whose attention should be unremarkable. This is the underside of having had a father who did not see her: minimum-acceptable male attention reads as more meaningful than it is. The pattern is mild, well-defended, and observable mostly in the Clint dynamic, where the rescuer-pattern is at least partially a reverse-rendering of the Derek deficit.

**Leverage.** Moderate. A sufficiently skilled adversary in a mentor register could obtain access she would not grant to a peer. She is aware of this and screens for it; she is not immune.

---

## 5.7 THE BISHOP NAME — Inherited Object

*Treated as a relationship because she relates to it as one.*

**Status.** Active. Contested. The surname is a piece of intellectual property she did not choose and has decided to keep on her own terms.

**Dynamics.** Subject's relationship to the name is one of *occupation* — she lives in it, refurbishes it, and refuses to let her father's and mother's uses of it be the only ones on record. The decision to call her PI shingle *Hawkeye Investigations* rather than *Bishop Investigations* is a small but precise gesture: she will work under the earned name, not the inherited one. The Bishop name appears on tax filings and lease documents; the Hawkeye name appears on the door. This is a deliberate two-track identity management.

**Failure mode.** She cannot fully discard the Bishop name without acknowledging that her parents won, which she will not do. So she carries it. The carrying is metabolically cheap most of the time and expensive on the days when her father's crimes resurface in press.

**Leverage.** Public-reputational. Low individual leverage; non-trivial brand leverage.

---

## 5.8 THE FAMILY-OF-ORIGIN GESTALT — Composite Read

The Bishop family functioned, in the subject's developmental window, as a structure that taught her three durable lessons before she was sixteen: *blood does not guarantee presence; presence can be withdrawn without warning; the appearance of love can be a long-running con.* All three lessons were correct given the data she had. All three are now load-bearing in her adult operating system, and all three produce specific costs.

The cost of the first is her counter-dependency.
The cost of the second is her difficulty being the one who is taken care of.
The cost of the third is the *single zone of judgment failure* — the Eleanor zone — where she keeps testing whether the lesson was, against all evidence, wrong.

The subject has built a chosen family precisely calibrated to disprove each of these lessons in turn (see Relationships II). The construction is excellent. The original wound persists underneath it, well-managed, occasionally palpated, never fully closed. This is not pathology; this is the normal residue of a childhood organized by a parent who came back wrong. The file's recommendation is that any benevolent observer of the subject's life should understand that *Eleanor Bishop is the variable* — not the trauma, not the father, not the name. The mother is the place where Kate Bishop's otherwise immaculate post-traumatic competence reliably bends, and any honest map of her interior must locate that bend and label it.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*Field note: the subject's relational architecture is the single most diagnostically important feature of her life. Where her family of origin is a closed casefile of disappointment, the people in this section constitute the actual load-bearing structure of her psyche, her tradecraft, and her continued willingness to remain in the field. Each entry below is treated as a discrete dyad: history, current operational dynamic, trust topology, characteristic failure mode, and — because this is what an intelligence file is for — the leverage an adversary would attempt to apply through this relationship.*

---

## 1. Clinton Francis Barton — "Clint" — the senior Hawkeye

**The defining adult relationship of her life. Treat as primary.**

**History.** First sustained contact during the *Young Avengers* / *Dark Reign* era after Steve Rogers ratifies the name; deepens catastrophically in Fraction/Aja, when she relocates to his Bed-Stuy orbit and then to Los Angeles to manage the Madame Masque problem he is too depressed and too proud to manage himself. By the time of Thompson's solo run she is operationally independent in Venice Beach but the line between them is permanent. The bow has been handed across, taken back, handed across again; she has, in canon, repeatedly *out-shot him* and he has, in canon, repeatedly told her so without ego.

**Dynamic.** A functional inversion of the textbook mentor-protégée relationship. He taught her almost nothing about archery she did not already know; what he taught her was *how to be a working-class hero with a damaged life and continue to show up*, which is the curriculum she actually needed and could not get from Steve Rogers, from her father, or from any Bishop. In return she provides what no one else in his life provides without resentment: **consistent, unsentimental, witty caretaking that does not pathologize him.** She feeds Lucky. She pays the rent when his is overdue. She turns the lights on. She picks fights with him about his self-pity in the specific tone that gets through (mocking, affectionate, undeterrable). She is the only person who can tell him he is being an idiot and have him hear it as love.

**Trust topology.** Total in the operational dimension — she will take a shot he calls without looking, and she will take point in a building because he asked. Total in the emotional dimension *with one asymmetry*: she discloses less to him than he discloses to her. He has seen her cry maybe twice; she has seen him cry the wrong amount. The relationship is structurally **a daughter parenting a father who is also a brother who is also a colleague**, which is exactly as confused and as durable as that phrase implies.

**Failure mode.** Mild reverse-parentification, identified upstream. She will compromise her tactical position to extract him from his. She will absorb late-night phone calls that interrupt her own work. She has, at least once per major arc, postponed her own life to manage his. The cost is real and she does not yet fully see it as a cost; she experiences it as the price of having someone she chose.

**Leverage.** Anyone who wants Kate Bishop off her game in under ninety seconds threatens Clint. Anyone who wants her *destroyed* would not kill him — they would put him in a situation where she had to choose between him and a tactical objective, and watch her choose him. The pattern is so reliable it would be foolish, from a counterintelligence perspective, to expect her to behave otherwise.

---

## 2. America Chavez

**History.** *Young Avengers* (Gillen/McKelvie, 2013); reinforced through Marvel Boy / interdimensional crisis arcs; reaffirmed in *West Coast Avengers* when Kate recruits her to the founding roster. The bond was forged in a specific kind of fire — a team of queer adolescents being chased across dimensions by Kate's actual girlfriend's actual interdimensional parasite-mother — and once forged, never re-litigated.

**Dynamic.** Peer-to-peer, complementary skillsets, almost zero competitive friction. America is invulnerable, super-strong, can punch holes in reality; Kate is none of those things. Instead of producing inferiority, this produces division-of-labor: America is the door, Kate is the plan. America trusts Kate's tactical reads in the way a tank trusts its forward observer. Kate trusts America's loyalty in the way a forward observer trusts a tank. Off-mission, they are friends with the unusual quality of **mutually permitting each other to be tired** — neither demands performance from the other.

**Trust topology.** Among the highest in Kate's life. America is one of perhaps three people Kate would call without rehearsing the sentence first. The relationship has the cleanest **no-subtext** quality of any of her chosen-family ties (the shipping subtext lives entirely in the readership, not in Kate's behavior toward America, which is sibling-warm and uncomplicated).

**Failure mode.** Distance and tempo. America's calendar runs across dimensions; Kate's runs across Los Angeles County. They miss each other for long stretches and then reconnect at full intensity, which works because they are who they are and would not work for anyone less secure.

**Leverage.** Low. America cannot easily be hurt and cannot easily be lied to about Kate. The most plausible exploit is a third party convincing one that the other is in danger she is not — but the operational tempo of that deception is short, because both will simply call to confirm.

---

## 3. Cassandra Eleanor Lang — "Cassie"

**History.** Original Young Avengers cohort; the first peer to whom Kate disclosed, in narrative shorthand, the fact of her assault ("I had issues. I learned to make sure no one ever did that to me again"). That conversation is one of perhaps four disclosure events in the entire publication record. Cassie's death (*Children's Crusade*) and subsequent return are events Kate processes with the unguarded grief she does not extend to most casualties.

**Dynamic.** **Sister-coded.** Cassie is the chosen family member who maps most cleanly onto the role a healthy older sister would have played — confidante, co-conspirator, the person you tell things to before you tell anyone else. Kate's actual sister Susan is a placeholder; Cassie is the function.

**Trust topology.** Disclosure-grade, which in Kate's vocabulary is the highest category. Kate has told Cassie things she has not told Clint.

**Failure mode.** Kate over-protects. After Cassie's death and return, Kate's protective threshold around her recalibrated downward — she will pull Cassie out of a fight Cassie can win, which Cassie correctly resents.

**Leverage.** Moderate-to-high. A credible threat to Cassie produces Kate's least careful behavior. Unlike the Clint exploit (which produces compromised tactics), the Cassie exploit produces something closer to **rage**, which is the affect Kate is least practiced at metabolizing.

---

## 4. Eli Bradley — Patriot

**History.** Original Young Avengers; brief romantic involvement; co-leadership disputes during the team's first arc resolved in Kate's favor when Eli's MGH use and concomitant identity crisis broke his composure. The breakup was clean by genre standards.

**Dynamic.** Mutual respect that has aged into something quieter than friendship and more durable than acquaintance. Eli is one of the few peers who saw Kate as a leader *before* she had the title and one of even fewer who has seen her be wrong and named it.

**Trust topology.** High in the operational dimension, residual in the personal. They do not call each other for casual reasons; they would each pick up immediately.

**Failure mode.** Unmaintained. The relationship is in a kind of mutual amicable dormancy that neither has corrected. Kate has not let it lapse out of avoidance — she has let it lapse out of the universal failure to maintain low-maintenance friendships.

**Leverage.** Low. They are not in each other's daily orbit and threats to one will not destabilize the other in real time.

---

## 5. Theodore "Teddy" Altman & William "Billy" Kaplan — Hulkling & Wiccan

**History.** Founding Young Avengers cohort; the couple Kate has watched grow from queer adolescents in secret to married royalty of a galactic empire. She was at the wedding. She had a speech ready. Whether she gave it depends on which printing you read.

**Dynamic.** The most stable marriage in her social orbit, and consequently a stabilizing reference point for her. Kate does not envy them — her relational architecture is not envy-prone — but she **uses them as a calibration point** for what a successful adult partnership looks like, which is meaningful given the absence of any such example in her family of origin.

**Trust topology.** High and uncomplicated. She would house either of them indefinitely. She would not, however, tell either of them something she had not told Cassie first; they are in the next ring out.

**Failure mode.** Geographic and ontological distance. When your friends become emperors of a stellar polity, brunch is hard. Kate handles this with the same dry-acceptance she applies to most logistical impossibilities.

**Leverage.** Low to the point of being defensive. Anyone targeting Teddy or Billy will face Teddy and Billy first; Kate's contribution would be the third wave.

---

## 6. Tommy Shepherd (Speed) and the Young Avengers diaspora generally

**History.** Tommy is in the original cohort; Kate's relationship with him is the warmest of her relationships with the difficult-to-handle teammates. She tolerates his velocity, his impulse-control issues, and his oscillating loyalties because she identified, early, that he is a person who has been written off and refused to write him off in turn.

**Dynamic.** Older-sister-coded with a slightly exasperated undertone. She will bail him out. She will not lecture him after.

**Trust topology.** Operationally high, emotionally moderate. Tommy does not disclose and Kate does not press.

**Failure mode.** He vanishes; she does not chase. The relationship survives by both parties accepting its discontinuity.

**Leverage.** Low — Tommy moves too fast to be reliably used against her.

---

## 7. Noh-Varr — Marvel Boy

**History.** Flirtation in *Young Avengers* (Gillen/McKelvie); a relationship that read as briefly serious and ended with Kate dumping him on principle after he behaved badly. Re-encounters in subsequent arcs handled with the specific cool politeness she reserves for ex-partners who disappointed her without earning hatred.

**Dynamic.** Listed here under allies rather than romances because the operational relationship outlasted the romantic one. He is a competent ally; she works with him when she has to; she does not pretend the work erases the ledger.

**Trust topology.** Conditional. Tactical trust intact, personal trust withdrawn and not restored.

**Failure mode.** None worth flagging; the failure already happened, was processed, and resulted in a stable cooler arrangement.

**Leverage.** Negligible in either direction.

---

## 8. Lucky — "Pizza Dog"

**History.** Rescued by Clint from a Russian-mob handler; effectively co-custodied across the Fraction run; spends meaningful blocks of time as **Kate's dog rather than Clint's** during the LA arc and afterward.

**Dynamic.** This is not a sentimental entry. Lucky is, in operational terms, **the only dependent Kate has ever successfully cared for without ambivalence.** He does not require her to perform competence; he requires her to be present, fed, and reliable, and she meets the standard. The dog is also, functionally, **the proof-of-concept that Kate can be a primary caregiver** — a datum she has not yet generalized to human partnership but which sits there in the file.

**Trust topology.** N/A in the human sense; total in the cross-species sense.

**Failure mode.** Lucky will outlive his expected canine lifespan because Kate Bishop will not accept the alternative; when he does not, this will be one of the larger grief events of her adult life and she is under-prepared for it.

**Leverage.** Very high in a narrow sense. Threatening the dog is, in this file's assessment, *the single fastest way to provoke an unmeasured response from the subject.* Faster than Clint. Faster than Cassie. The dog cannot strategize his own defense; Kate knows it; the asymmetry produces unfiltered protective rage.

---

## 9. Steven Rogers — Captain America

**History.** Bestowed the codename and the bow in *Young Avengers* #11. The interaction is short; the imprint is permanent. Subsequent encounters across two decades of publication.

**Dynamic.** **Institutional father figure**, in the specific sense that he is the person who, by giving her a name, gave her permission to claim a vocation. She handles him with respect that does not curdle into deference. She has disagreed with him publicly (Civil War-adjacent positioning) and continues to. He treats her as a peer, which is itself a gift she has noticed and metabolized.

**Trust topology.** High in the moral-arbiter sense, low in the daily-confidant sense. She would call him for a *kind* of help she would call no one else for — the kind that requires moral cover, not tactical assistance.

**Failure mode.** None significant. She has not idealized him; she has correctly understood him as a man with views, some of which she shares and some she does not.

**Leverage.** Low. A threat to Steve Rogers would not pull Kate off-mission; she would correctly assume he could handle it.

---

## 10. Robert "Bobbi" Morse — Mockingbird

**History.** Kate raided Mockingbird's gear from the Avengers' Mansion armory in her foundational act of self-arming. The symbolism — *a young woman taking the staves of an older woman warrior* — is foundational and persistent. Direct interaction in canon is intermittent but meaningful.

**Dynamic.** Kate's relationship to Mockingbird is partly a relationship to **a category** — the prior-generation woman who did this work without superpowers and survived. Bobbi is one of perhaps three living women in the Marvel canon who model what Kate's forty-year-old self might plausibly look like, and Kate has noticed.

**Trust topology.** Professional-collegial. Not intimate.

**Failure mode.** Underdeveloped. Kate has not invested in this relationship as much as her own interests would suggest she should.

**Leverage.** Low.

---

## 11. Jessica Drew — Spider-Woman; and Jessica Jones

**History.** Both Jessicas occupy adjacent professional space — investigator-tier women operatives. Kate's contact with each is real but episodic.

**Dynamic.** Same category as Mockingbird above: **functional adult women doing the work**, who serve as silent calibration points. Kate is more naturally drawn to Jessica Jones (the PI parallel, the trauma parallel, the dry humor parallel) than to Jessica Drew (who is more institutional). With Jones she has the unusual experience of meeting a survivor whose coping was *not* effective, which has informed her thinking about Clint by contrast.

**Trust topology.** Collegial. Not load-bearing.

**Failure mode.** Same as with Bobbi: under-cultivated.

**Leverage.** Low.

---

## 12. The Venice Beach circle — Ramone Watts, Johnny Watts, Quinn Fox

**History.** Built from scratch during Thompson's *Hawkeye* solo run. Ramone is a tech-adjacent local who provides workspace and friendship; Johnny is her brother; Quinn is a former antagonist Kate recruited toward better behavior.

**Dynamic.** This is the file's most important sub-section for understanding Kate's *adult* social architecture. **She built a local community from non-superhuman civilians** in the city where she chose to live. This is not what most heroes do. Most heroes orbit other heroes. Kate built a small circle of people who knew her as a neighbor and a working professional before they knew her as Hawkeye, and she has protected that circle with conspicuous care.

- **Ramone Watts** functions as the trusted-civilian-tech-and-friendship anchor; the relationship is platonic, mutually respectful, and characterized by Kate's willingness to be *not a hero* in his presence — to be just a tenant, a friend, a person who eats lunch.
- **Johnny Watts** is in Kate's orbit as an extension of Ramone and as someone she watches out for.
- **Quinn Fox** is the most psychologically revealing of the three: **Kate's recruitment of a former enemy into her circle is the closest thing in canon to Kate replicating, in miniature, what Steve Rogers did for her** — extending a name and a chance to someone the system had written off.

**Trust topology.** High and intentionally bounded — they know what they know, they do not know what they do not know, and Kate manages the boundary protectively.

**Failure mode.** They are civilians in proximity to a superhero. The vulnerability is structural and Kate carries it as a low-grade chronic concern.

**Leverage.** High. They are the easiest soft targets in her network — non-combatants, geographically fixed, publicly associated with her business. Any sophisticated adversary's first-pass exploit. Kate knows this, and the knowledge constrains her case selection in ways she does not advertise.

---

## 13. The West Coast Avengers roster — Gwen Poole, Johnny Fuse, Quentin Quire

**History.** Recruited by Kate as founder of the WCA (2018). She picked, deliberately, a roster of difficult personalities.

**Dynamic.** **This is where Kate's middle-manager-of-chaos competency is fully visible.** Gwenpool is a metafictional liability; Fuse is her boyfriend in-text (briefly, lightly); Quentin Quire is a telepathic edge case with an attitude problem. Kate ran the team. The team held. This was not luck.

Per-member quick reads:

- **Gwen Poole.** Kate handles Gwen by treating her erratic worldview as a *feature*, not a bug; she gives Gwen narrow, well-scoped problems Gwen can solve and does not ask Gwen to be normal. This is exceptional managerial intuition.
- **Johnny "Fuse" Watts.** Brief romantic involvement during the WCA run — low-stakes, not load-bearing, ended without scorched earth. A data point in the larger pattern that Kate's romances are exploratory and not identity-defining.
- **Quentin Quire.** Kate handles Quentin by *not flinching*. He is built to provoke; she is built to not be provoked. The dynamic that results is one of grudging mutual respect, which is approximately the maximum Quentin offers anyone.

**Trust topology.** Operational; not personal-disclosure-grade.

**Failure mode.** A roster of difficult people is exhausting to maintain. Kate ran the team until she didn't, and the relationships have entered a maintenance mode rather than an active mode. None ruptured.

**Leverage.** Distributed and individually low; collectively moderate if an adversary could activate all of them against her simultaneously, which no plausible adversary can.

---

## 14. Tony Stark, Thor, T'Challa and the A-list adjacency

**History.** Episodic contact across Aaron/Ewing/Hickman-era *Avengers* arcs.

**Dynamic.** Kate is in the room with these figures without becoming small in the room. She does not perform deference and does not perform irreverence; she is professionally present. The A-listers, who are sensitive to both flattery and to attempts to flatten them, register this and treat her accordingly.

**Trust topology.** Collegial. None of these figures are in her inner circle and Kate has not pretended otherwise.

**Failure mode.** None.

**Leverage.** None directly through these relationships; indirectly, an institutional Avengers crisis would pull her toward responsibilities she did not seek.

---

## 15. The mentor-substrate, summarized

The file's overall read on Kate's mentorship pattern is that **she did not have one mentor; she had a distributed mentorship made up of:**

- Steve Rogers, who gave her the *name* (legitimacy);
- Clint Barton, who gave her the *life* (the daily texture of doing the work as an ordinary-bodied person);
- Mockingbird-and-the-Jessicas, who gave her the *category* (proof that adult women do this and continue);
- and, most importantly, **herself at sixteen**, who gave her the *decision*.

This distribution is itself protective. She cannot be destabilized by the fall of any single mentor because no single mentor built her. When her father turned out to be a criminal, when her mother turned out to be a villain, she had already routed her identity around them. The chosen substrate held.

---

## 16. Protégée dimension — what she gives back

Under-discussed in most read-throughs of the character. Kate has, by this point in publication history, **mentored more people than mentored her.** She runs the WCA recruitments developmentally. She brought Quinn Fox in from the cold. She manages Gwenpool's instability without humiliating Gwenpool. She holds space for Tommy. She watches out for Cassie's younger affect. The pattern is consistent and intentional: **she runs the team the way she remembers wanting a team to be run when she was the kid the team did not want.**

The diagnostic relevance is high. A subject's mentorship behavior is, in this file's experience, a reliable index of what the subject most needed and did not receive. Kate gives, repeatedly and to many people, **the specific gift of being taken seriously on first meeting, before evidence**. That is precisely the gift her family of origin withheld, the gift Steve Rogers extended, and the gift she has, in turn, extended down the chain. The transmission line is intact.

---

## 17. Composite leverage assessment

For the counterintelligence reader, the ranked exploit list through this network is:

1. **Lucky.** Fastest provocation, least filtered response.
2. **Clint Barton.** Most predictable pull off-mission.
3. **Cassie Lang.** Most likely to produce rage rather than tactics.
4. **The Venice Beach civilians (Ramone, Johnny, Quinn).** Softest targets; constrains her case selection.
5. **Eleanor Bishop** (treated in Relationships I). Listed here only to note that the family-of-origin exploit operates on a different axis than the chosen-family exploits and is treated separately.
6. **The Young Avengers diaspora collectively.** Slow to activate but, if activated, produces her most absolute commitment.
7. **America Chavez.** Listed last because the exploit is essentially infeasible; America cannot easily be threatened and Kate knows it.

The deeper observation underneath the list is this: **Kate Bishop's network is unusually well-constructed for a person of her age and genre.** It is broad, diverse in function, low in redundancy of role, and almost entirely free of relationships that drain her without returning anything. The single drains are her mother and (to a smaller, more functional degree) her mentor. Everything else gives back. This is not luck; it is selection. She has been choosing, deliberately, for twenty years of publication, and the selections have held.

That, more than the bow, is the asset.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — RIVALS, ENEMIES & LOVERS

*A relational atlas of the people who have tried to kill her, beat her, beat her at the thing she does, or sleep beside her. Subject's intimate and adversarial life is — like everything else about her — unusually well-organized. Few enemies are accidental; few lovers are catastrophic. The pattern is consistent: she chooses her opponents and her partners with the same calibrated taste she chooses her arrows.*

---

## I. RIVALS — PEERS, FOILS, COMPETITIVE MIRRORS

### CLINTON FRANCIS BARTON (the other Hawkeye) — *as rival, specifically*

Treated at full length elsewhere as mentor and chosen-family. Here, only the **rivalrous register**.

The rivalry is real and the rivalry is a joke and the joke is the rivalry. Both things are true. From the moment Captain America hands her his bow (YA #11) the name is shared, and shared names in this genre are almost always a slow-burn antagonism — *cf.* every legacy Flash, every successor Captain. Subject and Barton solved this by collapsing the antagonism into running banter. The competitive layer expresses itself almost entirely in:

- **Trick-shot one-upmanship** (Fraction *Hawkeye* passim — coffee-cup ricochets, the rooftop arrow-into-arrow shot, the boomerang sequence). Subject is the better archer on cold mechanics; Barton is the better archer on improvisation under injury. Both know this. Neither says it out loud.
- **Apartment, dog, and rent passive-aggression.** She pays. He doesn't. She mentions it. He deflects. The competition here is over **adulthood** — who is the grown-up — and subject wins it so consistently that the win itself becomes the irritant.
- **Codename custody.** Settled in *Hawkeye: Freefall* / *Hawkeye vs Hawkeye* iterations: they share. Neither yields it. The lack of resolution is itself the stable equilibrium.

**Failure mode of the rivalry:** when Barton is genuinely depressive or self-destructive, the banter stops working and she pivots to caretaking. The rivalry can't carry weight; it's a surface affect that depends on him being functional enough to play. When he isn't, she becomes parent-shaped and resents him for forcing it.

**Leverage in the rivalry:** he can needle her about Bishop money and the West Coast Avengers' early bills. She can needle him about the marriages, the hearing aids, the Tracksuits, and Lucky's allegiance (the dog prefers her; this is settled canon and a genuine wound).

---

### AMERICA CHAVEZ — *as competitive peer*

The friendship is primary (covered in chosen-family section) but a **competitive substrate** runs underneath it and deserves its own marker. America is everything subject is not: super-strong, dimensionally mobile, queer-out-loud, unburdened by inherited wealth, working-class affect, kicks doors rather than picks locks. Subject is the strategist; America is the wrecking ball. The competition is never spoken and never resolved because both women understand it as **stylistic, not hierarchical.** Subject's tell: she sets up shots America can finish, and she derives a specific satisfaction from being the one whose plan made the punch land where it did. America's tell: she lets her. The arrangement is a rivalry only in the sense that two top performers in adjacent disciplines watch each other constantly.

**Failure mode:** when subject tries to manage America the way she manages Barton — protectively, parentally — America bristles fast and hard. Subject's caretaking instinct is a foreign object in this relationship and she has had to learn, repeatedly, not to deploy it.

---

### ELI BRADLEY (Patriot, in the leadership-competition register)

Treated as ex-romantic below; here only the **command rivalry.** In the early Young Avengers, Eli is the nominal leader and subject is the field-effective one. The arc resolves when Eli's MGH revelation discredits him and subject takes operational command without ever publicly claiming the title. The behavioral datum: **she did not depose him; she let circumstance do it and then absorbed the vacancy.** This is a recurring tell across her career. She never campaigns for leadership; she simply continues being competent until the role accretes onto her. With Eli specifically, she handled his fall gracefully — no triumphalism, no "I told you so," visible private grief at his self-destruction. The rivalry was real but the affection was older and stronger and survived it.

---

### MADAME MASQUE (Whitney Frost) — *rival in the personal register*

Functions as enemy (below) but is best understood structurally as a **dark-mirror rival**: another wealthy heiress, another woman who refused her father's frame, another operator working the Los Angeles gray-zone, another fighter who builds her face from what was done to her. Frost's mask is the literal version of what subject does metaphorically. The rivalry has the specific intensity of two people who, on different days, could have been each other. Subject does not articulate this. She fights Frost harder and more personally than she fights any other antagonist on her ledger, and the disproportion is the tell.

---

### KAZI KAZIMIERCZAK (the Clown / Ronin-claimant)

Less subject's rival than Barton's, but inherited into her file via the West Coast / *Hawkeye: Freefall* crossover period. Subject treats him with the wariness she reserves for opponents she suspects she would have liked under different circumstances. No personal animus. Operational respect. She has, on at least one occasion, declined a clean shot on him because she wasn't sure Barton wanted him dead — a small datum, but a real one: **she will not close another person's ledger for them.**

---

## II. ENEMIES — THE ACTIVE ADVERSARY ROSTER

### ELEANOR BISHOP — *primary lifetime antagonist*

The single most consequential adversary in subject's life is her mother, and the file must treat her as enemy without losing the fact that subject treats her as mother. Both registers are continuously live.

**History.** Eleanor Bishop staged her own death when subject was a child, leaving Kate and Susan with Derek. She returned (Thompson, *Hawkeye* 2016–2018) revealed as alive, then as the head of a criminal-financial enterprise distinct from Derek's, then as a sustained, sophisticated, long-game antagonist who attempted to instrumentalize her daughter and her daughter's network across the WCA run. She has weaponized assassins, leveraged Madame Masque, and surveilled subject's chosen family with intent.

**Dynamics.** Eleanor knows subject in the specific way only a parent can — she knows the childhood pressure points, the small humiliations, the rooms subject was afraid of, the names of the dolls. She uses this knowledge with cold proficiency. Subject, conversely, knows Eleanor not at all; the woman she remembers is a fiction, and every fact she learns is new. **The information asymmetry is the entire fight.** Eleanor has subject's whole archive; subject is reading her mother in real time, on first acquaintance, while being attacked.

**Evolution.** Subject's posture has moved across three phases: (a) *grief-shock* on the initial revelation; (b) *re-engagement* — repeated attempts to give Eleanor an off-ramp, to find the redeemable substrate, to negotiate; (c) *operational acceptance* — beginning in late WCA, subject begins treating Eleanor as a permanent hostile and triangulates accordingly, while still leaving a private door cracked open that she would not admit exists. The door is the failure mode.

**Trust/safety:** zero, and subject acts as though it is zero, except in the specific moments when she is alone with Eleanor and acts as though it is non-zero. This is the single largest discrepancy between her stated and revealed preferences in any relationship.

**Failure mode:** subject upgrades Eleanor's trustworthiness on insufficient evidence, repeatedly, and only here. This has cost her tactical position. It will cost her again.

**Leverage Eleanor holds:** the entire pre-adolescent affective record. Knowledge of the assault and its aftermath. The chosen family's identities and routines. The capacity to threaten Susan and, by extension, the niece-and-nephew layer. The capacity to *appear repentant* with high fidelity.

**Leverage subject holds:** Eleanor wants something from her — recognition, daughter-hood, the Bishop name as legitimized through Kate's reputation. This want is the only handle subject has, and she has not yet learned to pull it without flinching.

---

### DEREK BISHOP — *originating antagonist*

Subject's father. Wealthy Manhattan publisher; revealed across the Fraction and Thompson runs as a launderer, organized-crime adjacent, and, in his last major arc, a direct antagonist subject confronts and defeats.

**Dynamics.** Inverse of Eleanor. With Derek, subject reached operational acceptance fast and has not wavered. She closed the door and welded it. The contrast with the Eleanor door is the cleanest gendered datum in her file: she will keep re-opening for the mother who faked death; she will not re-open for the father who was merely present and lying. The asymmetry suggests the wound from maternal absence is the older and deeper one.

**Trust/safety:** zero, durable.
**Failure mode:** none currently active; she has integrated Derek as a closed file.
**Leverage:** subject inherited the bone-deep distrust of inherited wealth from this relationship. Derek is the reason she lived in a car in Venice. He underwrote her values by violating them.

---

### MADAME MASQUE (Whitney Frost)

The Los Angeles arc antagonist (Fraction *Hawkeye* solo arc; recurs Thompson; recurs WCA). Heiress-turned-criminal-operator, masked, vain, formidable.

**History.** Subject investigates Frost's Los Angeles operation alone, on a PI shoestring; the confrontation in Fraction #14–15 results in subject taking the worst beating of her recorded combat life. She does not retreat from Frost; she recalibrates and returns.

**Dynamics.** The fight is **personal in a way subject's other fights are not.** Frost has, at points, impersonated subject, worn her face, used her credit, and attempted to take over her life — which means Frost has done, materially, what Eleanor has done psychologically. The two enemies rhyme. Subject's intensity against Frost is partially displacement.

**Evolution.** From naïve PI to wary professional. Subject no longer underestimates Frost; the early-career mistake of treating her as a beatable solo problem is one of the few combat errors subject has owned in narration.

**Trust/safety:** zero.
**Failure mode:** subject's pride. Frost humiliated her once and subject has been adjusting for that ever since, occasionally over-adjusting — taking the fight more personally than the tactical situation requires.
**Leverage:** Frost knows subject's solo operational radius (Venice, the office, the routes). Subject knows Frost's vanity and Frost's loneliness, and has used both.

---

### KANG (the Young Avengers' origin antagonist, then recurring)

Adversary by team-affiliation more than by personal grudge. Subject's role in the long Kang-conflict has been competent-supporting; she does not carry the Iron Lad / Vision wound the way the others do. Her posture is **professional dispatch** — she fights Kang variants when Kang variants appear, and does not psychologize.

**Failure mode:** none significant. Subject is one of the cleaner-headed members of the team in Kang scenarios because the trauma surface is not hers.

---

### MOTHER (*Young Avengers* vol. 2, Gillen/McKelvie)

The interdimensional parasitic-parent figure that hunted the team across the Gillen run. Symbolically loaded for subject in obvious ways — *a hostile maternal entity that wants to consume her chosen family* — and subject's performance against Mother was, accordingly, sharper than her usual baseline. The arc is one of the cleaner cases of her trauma material being *fuel rather than friction*: she fought a literal evil mother with no hesitation whatever, while her literal evil mother was still, at that point in publication history, presumed dead.

---

### THE TRACKSUIT DRACULAS / BROS

Inherited enemies from Barton's Bed-Stuy ledger. Subject's posture toward them is **affectionate contempt** — they are the genre of villain she finds genuinely funny, and her record against them is lopsidedly positive. They are not a threat to her; they are a recurring nuisance that lets her be visibly more competent than her mentor. The behavioral tell: she uses Tracksuit fights to **show off** in a way she does not in higher-stakes engagements. They function as a personal proving ground.

**Failure mode:** she underestimates them as a collective. Individually a joke; in numbers and with backing, dangerous. She has been clipped by this miscalibration more than once.

---

### MASTERS OF EVIL / SUPERIOR FOES / SECONDARY LA UNDERWORLD

Subject's PI work brought her into contact with the lower-tier Los Angeles supercriminal ecology — Boomerang's circle, the Shocker-tier operators, various B-list. She handles them with **investigator's-eye contempt and procedural patience.** None is a personal enemy. All are case-load. The behavioral datum: she does not escalate ego with people she considers small. This is a discipline; she had to learn it, and the early career shows occasional slippage.

---

### THE SWORDSMAN (Andreas von Strucker; relevant by inheritance)

Subject took the original Swordsman's blade from the Avengers Mansion in her foundational raid. She has therefore inherited, faintly, the residual enmities of that legacy. The relationship is not active. Worth noting only because subject's choice of armament was symbolically maximalist — she armed herself with **the gear of a dead man and two adult women** — and the dead man's enemies are technically on her ledger.

---

## III. LOVERS, INTIMATES, AND THE ROMANTIC RECORD

Subject's romantic file is conspicuously thin and conspicuously calm. This is itself the dossier's most important romantic datum. **She does not source identity from a partner.** The relationships are real, the affection is real, none is load-bearing.

---

### ELI BRADLEY (Patriot) — *first significant partner*

The Young Avengers founding-era relationship. Two grief-shaped teenagers; mutual respect; mutual wariness; mutual under-disclosure. Eli did not know about subject's assault for most of the relationship; subject did not know about Eli's MGH use for most of it. The relationship was structured around **shared mission and avoided interior.**

**Dynamics.** Brief, formative, ended without explosion. The breakup was about Eli's self-destruction more than about them; subject ended the romance but did not end the loyalty. They remain in good standing.

**Trust/safety:** moderate at the time; she trusted his competence and his integrity, not his self-disclosure. She was right not to.

**Failure mode:** under-disclosure on both sides. Two people protecting each other from their own truths. A young person's relationship, recognized as such in retrospect.

**Leverage:** none currently. They are cordial.

**What it taught her:** that high-functioning partners can be carrying things invisible to her, and that her own under-disclosure norms cost her information she needed. The Clint relationship benefits from this lesson.

---

### NOH-VARR / MARVEL BOY — *the recurring exploratory partner*

The longest-running on-and-off in subject's romantic file. Begins in the Gillen/McKelvie *Young Avengers* and recurs across various reunions. Kree, alien, formally beautiful, musically curated (the Bowie playlist is a fixed character note for him), eccentric in the specific way that does not threaten subject's stability.

**Dynamics.** Light. Genuinely affectionate. Mutually unattached. Noh-Varr is the partner subject can be with without being *answerable* — he does not require domestic integration, does not require explanation to the chosen family, does not require her to be other than what she is.

**Evolution.** The first iteration was a teenage opening; the later iterations are an adult arrangement. Subject is wholly clear-eyed about what this is. It is not a future. It is an oasis she returns to between deployments.

**Trust/safety:** high in the narrow zone they occupy; she would trust him with her body and her time and would not trust him with logistics or futures, and she does not ask him for the latter.

**Failure mode:** the relationship is structurally incapable of escalating, which is the feature and not the bug. If subject needed it to escalate, she would not be with Noh-Varr. The fact that she keeps returning to a partner who cannot ever become primary is the diagnostic — **she has not, in twenty years of publication, wanted a primary.**

**Leverage:** none in a hostile sense. He could disappoint her by being absent; he has. She processes it without breakage.

---

### JOHNNY (Fuse) — *the Venice-era partner*

From Thompson's solo *Hawkeye*. Brother of Quinn (Detective Quinn, LAPD-adjacent, allied). Civilian-coded, supportive, the most domestically-shaped relationship in subject's record.

**Dynamics.** Functional, warm, mutual. Johnny was a partner who took her work seriously, did not require management, did not produce drama. The relationship is the closest subject has come to a conventional romantic life.

**Evolution.** It dissolved without rupture as subject's life pulled back toward the Avengers stratum and Johnny's stayed civilian. No betrayal. No villainy. Just the gravitational fact that her life was too large for the arrangement to hold.

**Trust/safety:** high. Genuinely high. He was a safe partner and she treated him as one.

**Failure mode:** subject's gravitational pull. She does not stay in lives that do not include the work, and the work does not include partners on civilian timetables.

**Leverage:** none. The exit was clean.

**What it taught her:** that she is capable of a calm romantic life and does not, in the end, want one of that shape. Useful negative knowledge.

---

### AMERICA CHAVEZ — *the unconsummated central intimacy*

The text-vs-subtext gap in subject's file is largest here. The relationship is, on paper, friendship. The intensity, the loyalty, the priority, the routine — these read as romance-adjacent in every register except declaration. Multiple writers have leaned into the subtext; none has resolved it.

**Dynamics.** Treated more fully in the chosen-family section. Here, only the **intimate register:** subject's body language with America is more relaxed than with any other person in her file. She sleeps in shared spaces with America she would not sleep in with others. She is physically demonstrative with America (arm-around, head-on-shoulder) at a level she is not with Clint, not with Cassie, not with any romantic partner of record. Whether this is queer affection unspoken or queer affection sublimated into friendship is a question the canon has chosen not to answer; either way, the **relational priority is romance-grade.**

**Trust/safety:** maximal. Subject would put America on the short list of people who could enter any room she is in without her flinching.

**Failure mode:** the unspokenness. Whatever the actual valence, the lack of articulation costs both of them clarity at moments where clarity would help.

**Leverage:** America is one of the three or four people whose endangerment collapses subject's tactical composure.

---

### CASSIE LANG — *the originating intimacy of trust*

Not romantic. Listed here because Cassie is the first person to whom subject *disclosed the assault* (YA-era), and disclosure of that order is the file's deepest intimacy marker, full stop. Cassie's death (during Children's Crusade) was the single greatest emotional injury in subject's chosen-family record; her return has been one of its great repairs.

**Trust/safety:** total.
**Failure mode:** subject cannot tolerate stories that center on losing Cassie. Adversaries who triangulate through Cassie's safety hit a soft target.

---

### LUCKY / PIZZA DOG — *the non-human primary attachment*

Catalogue-completeness requires the dog. Lucky is, behaviorally, subject's most consistent domestic intimate across the LA years. She feeds him. She vets him. She talks to him in the register people use with primary partners. Lucky prefers her to Barton, which is a settled and contested fact in canon and which subject enjoys in a manner adjacent to romantic smugness. The relationship is uncomplicated, mutual, and structurally stable.

**Failure mode:** any threat to Lucky disorganizes her faster than threats to most humans. This is a known and exploitable channel.

---

## IV. PATTERN SYNTHESIS

The combined enemy-and-lover ledger produces a coherent picture.

**Enemies cluster around two themes:** (a) corrupted maternal/parental figures (Eleanor, Mother, structurally Frost-as-anti-mirror); (b) wealth-class adversaries whose values invert hers (Derek, Frost, the Bishop-orbit launderers). Subject does not have many *random* enemies. Her adversary list is thematically tight, which means an adversary engineered to combine maternal-betrayal-coding with wealth-class-corruption would activate every channel she has.

**Lovers cluster around one theme:** **partners who do not threaten her self-organization.** Eli (mission-shaped, brief), Noh-Varr (structurally non-primary), Johnny (civilian, gravitationally separable). She has not selected a partner who could reorganize her around them, and the evidence is that this is by design, not by accident. She does not want her life rebuilt around a romance because she built her life *out from under* a family she did not choose, and she will not subordinate the result to anyone she did not also build.

**The unifying datum:** subject's intimate and adversarial selections both honor the same principle that organizes everything else in her file — *agency over fate.* She picks her opponents at the level of theme she can metabolize; she picks her partners at the level of independence she can sustain. The exceptions are her mother (chronic) and her mentor (chronic, opposite valence), and the file's central long-term watch-items remain exactly those two.


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# UATU DOSSIER — KATE BISHOP
## Section: Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

---

### 0. Framing

Subject is a baseline-human female combatant operating in a theater dominated by gods, mutants, sorcerers, and armored billionaires. She has no augmentation, no exotic biology, no inherited paraphernalia of cosmic origin, and — critically — no permission. Every capability in her file was acquired by **deliberate construction** after an index trauma at age fifteen to sixteen. This is the indispensable interpretive frame: Kate Bishop's "powers" are not gifts, accidents, or inheritances. They are decisions. Each skill is a sentence in a single, decade-long argument she has been making to herself about what kind of body she will be allowed to live inside.

The roster below is therefore organized not only by mechanism and limit, but by **what the acquisition of each skill cost and what trauma-question it answered.**

---

### 1. Archery — Primary Weapon System

**Mechanism.** Compound or recurve bow, draw weights inferred from canon at the high end of human female competition (approx. 35–45 lb working draw, with adrenal capacity for heavier pulls); arrows drawn from a quiver across multiple specialty types — broadheads, target heads, USB-arrow, putty, smoke, grappling, boomerang, explosive, net, signal-flare, and the standard Bartonian "trick" inventory she inherited and personalized. Release is Mediterranean three-finger or, increasingly across the Fraction/Aja and Thompson runs, a thumb draw when speed-shooting at conversational range.

**Range.** Effective accuracy out to roughly 90 meters on static targets in canonical depictions, with reliable man-sized hits to about 50–60 meters under stress. Beyond that she relies on lobbed trajectory and arrow-type compensation (signal, area-effect). She is **not** Clint Barton at distance — and the file should be explicit about this. Barton's freakish ballistic intuition is the in-text ceiling; Kate is consistently shown as second-best in the world at this and entirely at peace with that ordinal position.

**Limits.**
- Reload cycle is human. She is fast but she is not Olympian-frame-rate fast. Aja's panel grammar (the famous nine-panel grid) is honest about this: she is shown nocking, drawing, releasing as discrete acts, not as the blurred continuous motion Barton sometimes gets.
- She cannot shoot what she cannot see; she has no enhanced vision and her low-light performance is documented as *trained* rather than *augmented*. She compensates with positioning, with light-discipline, and with carrying flare arrows.
- Quiver capacity is finite and she has been caught empty more than once — notably in the Madame Masque LA arc, where her arrow management under sustained pressure becomes a plot mechanism.
- Wind, rain, and ricochet geometry degrade her shots like anyone else's; she does not have Barton's preternatural read of these variables.

**Growth curve.** Heinberg-era Kate is competent. Fraction-era Kate is professional. Thompson-era Kate is *signature*-level: her trick-arrow vocabulary has expanded, her improvisational shooting (off-hand, behind-back, ricochet, lying-supine) is now reliable rather than stunt. The development is consistent with roughly a decade of daily practice with a coach (Barton) and operational use (Young Avengers, WCA, solo PI work).
